Dr. Verleger, I very much appreciated your testimony on the Strategy Petroleum Reserve because I, too, believe that is a factor that we can influence, that has some potential to affect prices.
You say in your testimony that the current oil price increase has not been spurred by speculation. And I just want to make sure I'm understanding your testimony. I seems that you and Dr. Krapels have come to different conclusions. Is that correct?
VERLEGER: I'm not exactly sure where Ed is on that. And I -- one of the problems, as the CFTC noted in its looking at the large trader activities is it's very easy to look at the same data and reach two different conclusions.
What I looked at is since August. I think I first followed this data in 19 -- an academic article in '85.
Since August 18th a couple things have happened. One is we have a financial crisis. And as part of that financial crisis we've seen really people look for any sort of asset that's liquid that they can price. Like the special SIVs and special investment vehicles and so on.
And so to a certain extent I think there's been downward pressure. I think we will learn later when we get the data there's downward pressure on prices from speculators as financial organizations scramble for liquidity. This is something hat Charles Kindleberger (ph) wrote about in "Manics, Panics and Crises" years ago, and it's something I guess many of my classmates at MIT have studied and I've been following.
So to look -- as I said, we've had this very big price increase. And this -- speculation just doesn't fit with this right now because the people -- those people who are long oil or long physical assets are desperate for cash and they're going to treasury bills.
And I think in this cycle, this particular time, I don't think we're going it's speculation that did it. There are pension funds that are buying assets. And that's what Dr. Krapels and I were talking about in Vienna.
And those pension funds have continued to buy assets. Harvard has bought assets and so on. They view those as a better return than say buying Exxon stock and it's perfectly legitimate.
But that's not speculation. These are people who own stock and stay in that asset for long periods of time. They may have to roll their futures positions every month. They may be -- they may choose to buy oil and then just every month replace the futures contract.
But that's not speculation; that's investment. And that's a new form of investment.
COLLINS: Thank you.
Dr. Krapels?
KRAPELS: I completely agree with Phil. I don't think we're in a different position here. And I think the use of the word speculation tends to narrow the discussion.
When a pension fund decides to buy a lot of oil contracts as an ongoing strategic acquisition of assets and intends to hold those contracts for years and years and just simply rolls them over, is that speculation? It is, but it's not a speculative organization that's doing it because it's likely representing people that you wouldn't typically associate with speculation.
So, one of the things I tried to share with you in my written testimony is that there are four or five different types of financial entities engaging in this market. It's created a hypersensitive situation.
If delta hedging is having the effects that Phil describes, then what we really have here is just a hypersensitive market created by the very size of the positions that hedgers and speculators constantly deal with.

That -- from 2004 to 2006 we benefited because that converted backwardation to Contango and that promoted inventory building, so that a year ago Morgan Stanley was holding a great deal of heating oil in New York Harbor and earning a good return on this. This is what Cargill became famous on, that you'd buy at $20 and you'd sell forward in the futures market at $40 and you earn a return of -- if that's a year at 100 percent.

Do you think that OPEC has purposefully created an inventory tightness and continues to keep their production, or is production at levels that deprive the markets of our ability to build inventories?
CARUSO: I think it was definitely their goal when they reduced quotas late '06 and the beginning of '07. There were two reductions of OPEC targeted production levels in the fall of '06 and another one in the early part of '07 mainly because they saw that inventories in the U.S. and in other OECD countries were relatively high relative to the five-year average.
And they saw prices coming down in the latter part of '06 and the beginning of '07, prices have got into the $50, $60 range. This clearly was, in OPEC's view, price that they would like to have seen increased. And that's why they reduced production.
And from the period of the fourth quarter of 2006 through the third quarter of 2007, OPEC production was reduced by about 800,000 barrels a day, leading to lower inventories and a tight -- very tight market, leading, we believe, to most of the price increase.
MURKOWSKI: In the aftermath of Katrina, we know that there were refineries that were offline, and we know that there were wells in production that were taken off at that time. Are we at 100 percent now after Katrina in terms of those wells that were producing prior to, are we missing anything domestically then in terms of our ability to produce domestically?
CARUSO: Well, the oil and gas production in the Gulf of Mexico is increasing, but it's still below the level that would have been expected had not -- had the major hurricanes had not occurred. So we're ...
MURKOWSKI: But is everything online?
CARUSO: ... we're a bit below. Some decisions were made not to bring certain wells and other facilities back online because the cost was believed to have been prohibitive, given the revenues that could be earned by bringing that back online. The cost benefit decision was made to leave some of that production offline, and it delayed the new production in some fields, including Thunder Horse, which of course was damaged by the hurricane directly.
MURKOWSKI: But in terms of significant amounts, would you describe that as being significant to what we're seeing in our inventories now?
CARUSO: I'd say it was important, but clearly not nearly a major driver, about several hundred thousand barrels per day lower than it would have been.
MURKOWSKI: Let me ask you a little bit different tack here. This was in your written testimony, and you also mentioned briefly in your oral testimony here the impact of the U.S. dollar, recognizing that we're seeing a decline there, adding to continued oil consumption, because oil is trading in U.S. dollars, making the increase in the price of oil less severe on foreign economies.
Is it likely that oil prices might move from dollar pricing to being based on some other currency? I know that this was discussed in some recent OPEC meetings.
You look at European nations. You look at Japan, with very strong currencies of their own. Is that price of oil affecting them, as we are seeing here? Just give me a little discussion on whether or not we will continue the direction that we are currently on in terms of the oil pricing.
CARUSO: The appreciation of other currencies relative to the dollar has meant that the costs to the consumers in the euro zone, in the yen in Japan and even in other currencies the full cost of the price increase is not being borne by the consumers in those areas. Therefore, that has contributed somewhat to increase in demand because it's a lower real price.
It's also contributed to, I believe, thinking and the discussions in the OPEC meetings that have been reported, that OPEC ministers have said their revenues, in effect, are buying less because of the purchasing power loss. And so, it's certainly possible at that part of the decision-making process within OPEC as well.
Whether it would lead to a change in the way oil is priced, I continue to believe that it will not because of the -- so many of the assets held by OPEC countries are in dollar-denominated assets that it would be detrimental to their own assets.
MURKOWSKI: Anybody else disagree?
GHEIT: But also, in OPEC, some of the countries have their own currency pegged to the dollar. And therefore, when they have their revenue come in dollar, and then they have to pay their cost where the foreign workers coming from Korea and elsewhere, these workers now are demanding to be paid either in euro or their own country currency, because the money they are sending home is really less than before because of the depreciation of the U.S. dollar.
So there's tremendous pressure on OPEC. I was in Dusseldorf last week, and there was a TV interview with the Algerian Oil Minister, who said that, "I wish we had known about the drop in the dollar. We would have thought about switching in other currency before the fact." But obviously, if you flood the dollar, again, as the euro, for example, is down almost 40 percent in the last couple of years here.
So you are going to see additional pressure, upward pressure on the oil price as a result of the decline in the U.S. dollar.
MURKOWSKI: Thank you, Mr. Chairman. I have no more questions.
LEVIN: Thank you.
Let me get back to the NYMEX contract for a minute. We were talking -- Senator Murkowski and others were talking about valid benchmarks, and the benchmark, which is used for the NYMEX price, is the Cushing, Oklahoma price. Now, that price can be affected, I take it, when we're using sweet crude for the SPR. Dr. Verleger, that's basically the heart of your testimony, is it not?
VERLEGER: Yes. Now, when they say the NYMEX contract is the -- well, it's actually sweet crude. It's actually light sweet crude.
LEVIN: Light sweet crude.
VERLEGER: There are a number of crude oils that can be delivered against the NYMEX contract, and the list has changed over the years. But the NYMEX as so that to expanded the deliverable, for example, a Brent car -- a Brent could be delivered into Houston and then piped up -- it has to be moved into Cushing unless an alternative delivery procedure is agreed to by both the long and the short.
But there are a number of crudes, and they're all kind of light sweet. Many of them are also on the list of sweet crudes that quality for submission to the DOE's West Hacksbury (ph) -- West Hackberry (ph), where they keep sweet crude in the strategic petroleum reserve.
LEVIN: OK. Would it be wise for the SPR to use a greater percentage of non-sweet -- I guess sour crude, in terms of price?
VERLEGER: That is in my testimony, yes.
LEVIN: That's the heart?
VERLEGER: Yes.
LEVIN: It's part your testimony?
VERLEGER: The heart of my testimony.
LEVIN: All right.
Now, Mr. Caruso, why is the DOE not doing that? CARUSO: My understanding, as I'm not in the policy-making, but my understanding is the way the crudes are chosen is to try to have the best mix that fits our refinery configurations in this country, and that's what the strategic petroleum reserve office has used as a criteria for that.
LEVIN: All right.
So now, Dr. Verleger?
VERLEGER: I would have said the Treasury, when we created this in the '70s, in the '70s we had a number of refineries that could only process sweet crude, and they developed a number of facilities. There are a number of -- if you would read the international energy programs, there are a number of different storage salt domes.
And we put sweet crude in those, and that's 30 years ago. Now we have moved to a situation where more refiners can process heavier crudes.
We also have gone to these very tight environment specifications, which could, in an emergency, be relaxed, as they were after Katrina. And yet, to my knowledge, there has been no study as to whether the mix of crudes we are putting into the reserve today is appropriate, given today's refining standards.
LEVIN: Or the capability of taking action if they're not perfectly reflective of refinery standards.
VERLEGER: Right, yes.
LEVIN: Either one.
Now, Mr. Caruso, why doesn't the DOE make that change to save American consumers some money?
CARUSO: I'm not aware why they have not. I do know that the current mix of fill is one-third light sweet and two-thirds heavy sour.
LEVIN: We set in law -- well, I know that. we know that, but that's apparently the way it's been for some time. Is it -- change that mix?
CARUSO: Well, next June, the material that this committee's staff provided me said that it's going to be two-thirds sweet crude, one-third sour crude, in the first half of this next year.
LEVIN: Now, why is that being done?
CARUSO: I would have to answer that for the record because of it's the decision made by the office within DOE that runs the strategic petroleum reserve.
LEVIN: Yes. Would you find that out, sir? I mean, we've been pressing this point. We have a law which says that you've got to fill the reserve in a way that minimizes the cost. Looks to me like the DOE is ignoring the law, Senator Collins pointed out, but also ignoring the pocketbook of Americans.
This is a reserve. This isn't oil that we are going to have to refine. It's oil that someday we may have to refine, or we may have to waive environment laws to refine it.
But if an emergency is such that we have to take oil out of the reserve, why would the DOE, number one, ignore the law, but number two, ignore the pocketbooks of Americans?
CARUSO: I will take that question back to the Department, Senator.
LEVIN: If we could -- there's a chart that's up there. This is the price of oil at Cushing, and this shows the relationship, at least at Cushing, of supply and demand. And we show here that, when the demand goes up, when the supply goes down, the price goes up, relates inventories to price.
Mr. Caruso, can you see that chart?
CARUSO: Yes, sir.
LEVIN: Does it make sense for us to be increasing the -- or excuse me -- decreasing the supply at Cushing of sweet crude by putting that sweet crude in the SPR when the direct effect of what we're doing is increasing the price at Cushing, Oklahoma, which has a direct impact on the NYMEX price, which has a direct impact on future contracts? Does that make sense to you? Or do you disagree with that?
CARUSO: The facts are the facts. I mean, those are the facts that are on the chart. I don't disagree with the facts of the ....
LEVIN: You believe that supply and demand is the thing that is the most controlling in terms of the cost of oil? You have argued this morning about ignoring the impact of speculation.
But here at one location where there's not speculation, there's direct supply and direct demand, we see the relationship of price to supply. You're a big believer in that as being the cause, instead of speculation in the general market.
Why in the name of heaven would the DOE not follow the law to reduce that cost to Americans? Why would we want to increase the price of oil at Cushing, Oklahoma? I know you'll take it for the record.
Well, you're an expert at this. You make assessments of energy prices. Can you give us any idea, from your perspective, why they would do that?
CARUSO: Well, obviously, the decision was made based on a number of (inaudible) factors.
LEVIN: But why would that decision be made? Do you know? Do you have any idea?
CARUSO: I can't answer that question.
LEVIN: All right. We'll get off that.
Senator Murkowski, do we ...
MURKOWSKI: Go ahead, Mr. Chairman. I'm finished with my questions.
LEVIN: OK.
Should we -- and I can ask all of you -- most of you this question. Should we encourage the NYMEX, which does a terrific job I think most people can see, should we encourage them to broaden what their benchmark is? This is something also Senator Murkowski asked.
Should we encourage them to not just look at Cushing, Oklahoma, but broaden it? I guess the main determiner of that price, which has got such a huge impact on the future contract prices, is Cushing, Oklahoma.
Dr. Krapels, let me start with you.
KRAPELS: Creating a successful futures market is really, really difficult, and I think they have tried again and again and again to create a benchmark sour crude contract, and they have failed. There is simply that alchemy that they somehow got the WTI to get working in the 1980s has never repeated itself in any other crude contract other than Brent, which looks like and smells like WTI.
I'm not sure Phil would agree with this, but there has recently been an effort to create a sour crude futures market in the Middle East. I don't think it's going to succeed. The reasons for it we'd probably need a whole new set of hearings to discuss it. It's extremely difficult to create a successful futures market.
LEVIN: A different benchmark.
VERLEGER: Well, I agree.
LEVIN: All right.
VERLEGER: But part of it is the delivery location. Futures markets work best when you have a number of producers and a number of consumers, and there's really no choke point.
And Cushing is unique in that it's where a number of pipelines come together, and there were a number of storage companies. And since NYMEX has been there, they've built more tanks.
I think that there is some hope. When pipeline reversals are finished and sour crudes are coming down from Canada, one may be able to move it, move and create a contract tied to the Alberta contracts.
The big problem, thought, is that you have so much financial interest now tied, you now have over two million -- I think over two million NYMEX-plus ICE look-alike contracts. And that's just momentum, and that is just -- one of the things they'll tell you if you're marketing futures contracts is the first exchange to be successful wins.
LEVIN: Would it be possible to broaden the delivery points? Isn't that what you were suggesting?
VERLEGER: Oh, I was going to change the kind of crude.
LEVIN: Well, we can't change the crude.
VERLEGER: Well, no. If you deliver South (ph), and so you could change it to a sour contract in Cushing, which would change things. There are a number of delivery points available on this.
The other thing is to go to a cash settlement contract. Brent is cash settlement with no delivery point. And it wouldn't have worked in 1983 because no one believed energy was a commodity.
Now that's not a problem, so you could go to a cash settled contract off of a series of indices, and that would work very well. And that would take some of the distortions that were identified here.
LEVIN: Let me go back to you, Dr. Gheit. You make reference in your testimony to raising the current margin requirement. What is the current margin requirement?
GHEIT: It's low (ph), right now, market. But trends are about 8 to 12 percent.
LEVIN: Eight to 12 percent?
GHEIT: Right.
LEVIN: And what is the margin requirement for stock at ...
GHEIT: Fifty percent.
LEVIN: ... that the SEC has set?
GHEIT: Fifty percent.
LEVIN: Fifty percent for stock.
GHEIT: Right.
LEVIN: This is 8 to 12 percent.
GHEIT: Correct.
LEVIN: Does that affect the amount of speculation?
GHEIT: Absolutely. What we're trying to do here is to slow down the traffic. We will put speed limit because we don't want people to get hurt. We're not saying that we should block the traffic. We should allow it to proceed, but it in a safe manner.
LEVIN: Now, the stock market has a lot of traffic, does it not?
GHEIT: Absolutely.
LEVIN: Even though it's got a margin requirement?
GHEIT: Absolutely.
LEVIN: And so, we don't want to (inaudible) more than -- we obviously want to have some traffic, as the doctor--you say, Mr. Gheit. Would you have a problem, Dr. Krapels, with increasing the margin requirement?
KRAPELS: I would not. I think it's an overdue idea.
LEVIN: Could you comment on that?
KRAPELS: Well, I think for exactly the reasons Mr. Gheit has mentioned, we tend to get over-leveraged in these markets. Big traders, especially speculative hedge funds, using the amount of leverage that they use, it magnifies their impact on price.
I'm not sure 50 percent is the right number, but something well north of where we are right now seems to me like good public policy.
GHEIT: One other thing ...
LEVIN: Anyone else?
VERLEGER: It seems to me that both Ed and Mr. Gheit are right. The one point to add is that all these passive investors that are coming in, that have bought into this market, essentially have 100 percent margin because they set aside, when they buy the commodity contract, they set aside.
So the liquidity is there in the market already. And I think, in terms of reducing speculation, there's a longstanding history in financial markets where you raise the margins and you reduce the speculation. Whether it changes the behavior of prices, I'm not sure.
LEVIN: OK.
GHEIT: But also, if we changed, if the NYMEX were to switch into other types of crude, I do believe that will have a negative impact on WTI, and will just pull it down sharply.
We need to burst the bubble, whether it's going to come from economic slowdown or government action. But I feel that it's like 24/7 open gambling hall that people think that nobody will get hurt.
But that was the subprime. That was the SML. And all these things a lot of people think is going to be a win-win. There will be no casualties here. The fact of the matter, it's different, and I don't believe that that is -- this party will last forever. It will come to an end.
And I think the sooner the better, because the longer it stays, it's really going to disturb and disrupt future capital spending because, right now, a lot of oil companies will end up throwing in the towel, believing that oil prices of $100 are here to stay. And they will make investment decision that will regret sooner or later, and that is obviously going to hurt the oil industry. It's going to hurt supply-demand situation.
So it's not -- speculators are making money, but at a huge cost in the future to the economy, to the oil industry, to everybody. So few people will make a lot of money at the expense of very large number of people.
LEVIN: Many members of this panel believe in that very deeply. Not all of us, perhaps, or I can't speak for anybody else, but it's obvious that many of us think that this speculation has run wild. I think the chart on the amount of speculation demonstrates it.
I think you all either think that speculation has an impact on prices, obviously or clearly, or in the case of Mr. Caruso, a begrudging, perhaps. But nonetheless, that has been the subject of this hearing today.
And please, Senator Murkowski?
MURKOWSKI: Before we wrap up, I'm trying to get a better handle in my mind on how we define what a speculator is. You have a speculator that can affect prices, and then you have a speculator that can manipulate prices.
Tell me how we determine the difference, because I think it was you, Mr. Gheit, or maybe it was Dr. Krapels, mentioned when we were talking about the pension funds, and you hold these for 20 years. Is it speculation? Well, yes it is, but for a different purpose than one whose intent is to manipulate the markets.
And I think this is where so many of my colleagues get so upset and pound the table and say, "We need to do something about it" when we're actually manipulating the market. How do we define or make that distinction?
GHEIT: It's very gray area. The speculation and manipulation go hand-in-hand. You are not going to get an oil trader coming on television saying that he thinks oil prices will go down.
Why? It's not (inaudible). Everybody is in it now. It's like, if you can't beat them, join them.
And it's almost self-fulfilling prophecy. When somebody says oil prices will be $100 before the year-end, everybody is pushing for oil prices to cross the $100 mark.
MURKOWSKI: So are there any good speculators? KRAPELS: There are many good speculators. And I think you've asked the pivotal question, Senator. And I don't think you need to answer it. I don't think -- the one weakness of the report on Amaranth is that it is titled, "Excessive Speculation."
Now, there's a clear case for excessive speculation in the case of Amaranth, but it is so much on the margin of common practice that I think the solution to the problem that we're talking about, excessive volatility, tremendous hypersensitivity to prices, could be substantially addressed with higher margin requirements and much more information disclosure. If people knew what was going on, we wouldn't have the conspiracy theories that we have running around today.
So a lot can be fixed just with those two elements of the law, of the...
LEVIN: Let me give the definition from the CFTC. And I happen to agree with what you've said about difficulties of defining, but this is what the effort is by the CFTC. "Speculator does not produce or use the commodity, but risks his or her own capital trading futures in that commodity in the hopes of making a profit on price changes."
So I think the key to it is it's someone who isn't producing or using a commodity, but it's somebody who is buying and selling a piece of paper with no intent to use or produce it. Is that ...
KRAPELS: But millions of our citizens do it, so ...
LEVIN: Oh, no, no. Of course, no.
KRAPELS: There's no difference, really, between my purchase of General Motors' stock because I don't intend to use or make General Motors products.
LEVIN: But you do intend to keep it, the stock, presumably, unless you're...
CARUSO: But I also have a passive investment through a fund in futures, and there they hold oil. Now, some of them hold oil in the ground and some just buy futures, and the just -- they hold claims on oil. Perfectly legitimate academic financial definition, that they buy the oil and they just hold the position.
And when it matures, they have to -- they sell the position because they have to, and they take another long position. They stay subtly (ph) there.
And those--it's an investment. It's serving a very useful purpose because it promotes investment. And it doesn't cause this volatility.
LEVIN: Let me ask you a couple other of the -- you about a couple other suggestions here of Dr. Gheit, Mr. Gheit.
Current margin requirements, you've commented on that. Setting limits on the number of oil contracts by each account is another one of the suggestions at the end of your testimony, Mr. Gheit.
Dr. Krapels?
KRAPELS: Absolutely agree. I think looking at not just a current month but the out months as well, where Amaranth did a lot of its mischief, I think looking at the positions of individual traders, as the CFTC does today, applying that to ICE and monitoring it and enforcing rules is part of what they'll ...
LEVIN: OK. And Dr. Verleger?
VERLEGER: Absolutely.
LEVIN: All right, so we've got a third suggestion. Now, establishing a minimum holding period. Mr. Gheit suggested a minimum. Well, this gets to the question of the teacher's pension funds or something. They intend to hold that for a while.
VERLEGER: Yes. Well, they hold it. They roll the position.
LEVIN: They hold it.
VERLEGER: They hold it. They roll the positions forward.
LEVIN: But the fund that they're investing in doesn't intend to hold it for a particular period of time. They could buy and sell tomorrow or the next day constantly.
But does that have as much appeal to either of you as it does to Mr. Gheit?
CARUSO: Can I--the Goldman Sachs and the Dow Jones and these other funds actually continue holding it, based on the numbers of dollars they hold, that number of contracts, and they keep on holding it.
The holding period, when I heard Mr. Gheit say it the first time. I said that's a good idea.
The problem is that somebody who is speculating could take other offsetting positions. I think that is the regulation that's probably impossible to enforce.
LEVIN: OK.
KRAPELS: I agree.
LEVIN: Dr. Krapels.
Now, preventing conflict of interest by financial institutions is another of Mr. Gheit's suggestion. Give us an example, if you would.
GHEIT: Well, basically have the dozen or so largest investment banks in the world, and they are all involved very heavily in oil trading. But they are also clearinghouses. They also make investment into oil account. So basically, they can see your card. You can see theirs.
So they can see the traffic, whether it is going north or south, and they can put their money whether (inaudible) head of the people that put the orders through, and they can manipulate the price the way they want to see it.
And not long ago ...
LEVIN: With their own holdings?
GHEIT: With their own holdings, because they have a position. They can basically move the market daily if they want. And then is a momentum, and all of a sudden, you see everybody doing the same. Their program (inaudible) are all the same.
But what I have noticed, looking at what's happening, you read a statement (ph) in London or (inaudible) the action here in New York is almost fanning the flames. And again, it's self-fulfilling prophecy. We say oil prices.
One large investment bank not long ago said, although we still think on oil prices are still going to go higher, but we advise that some of you might wish to take money off the table. Guess what? The oil prices dropped by $4 in one day. There was no change in supply and demand.
The following day, oil prices regained the entire amount.
LEVIN: OK.
Dr. Krapels?
KRAPELS: No, I don't like that idea.
LEVIN: OK.
KRAPELS: I don't like how -- I think it's impractical.
LEVIN: On the conflict of interest issue?
KRAPELS: Conflict of interest issue.
LEVIN: Is it ...
KRAPELS: Not given the structure of our financial markets today.
LEVIN: Can't do it, OK.
The other one, stiff penalties on violators. I'll leave that one go.
Thank you all. You've been a terrific panel. We appreciate it all. And it's been a long hearing. We will stand adjourned.

Focus on Oil: China's price rise may affect electricity bills

Author: David Winning and Terence Poon

Publication info: David Winning and Terence Poon. 

https://ucd.idm.oclc.org/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/focus-on-oil-chinas-price-rise-may-affect/docview/308719065/se-2?accountid=14507

Abstract: The 10% increase in prices of gasoline, diesel and other fuels spurred investor enthusiasm Thursday for China's biggest oil companies, sending shares of fuel retailer China Petroleum & Chemical Corp., or Sinopec, up 9.4% in Hong Kong. The surprise announcement, made late Wednesday by China's top planning agency, was aimed at easing fuel shortages that have resulted from government price caps that kept fuel prices artificially low in the face of a global run-up in crude oil prices. The government bowed to pressure after shortages first started appearing in China's export-oriented south and quickly spread inland. China last adjusted domestic fuel prices 17 months ago, keeping them flat in the interim while international crude prices rose 30%. That meant that domestic refiners like Sinopec were forced to buy crude oil at ever higher prices, and sell it at the same low, government-set ceiling. As losses widened, some refiners stopped producing fuel, while others started hoarding in anticipation of a price increase because they weren't allowed to pass through the high costs to the consumers. Crude-oil prices were down midday Thursday in New York at $93.45 but threaten to pass $100 a barrel.

Links: https://JQ6AM9XS3S.search.serialssolutions.com?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Aeuropeannews&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;rft.atitle=Focus+on+Oil%3A+China%27s+price+rise+may+affect+electricity+bills&amp;rft.au=David+Winning+and+Terence+Poon&amp;rft.aulast=David+Winning+and+Terence+Poon&amp;rft.aufirst=&amp;rft.date=2007-11-02&amp;rft.volume=&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=8&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;rft.issn=09219986&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: BEIJING -- China's first fuel-price increase in more than a year is providing some much-needed relief to the country's refiners, but analysts warned it could lead to increases in other energy prices that could squeeze China's poorer residents.
The 10% increase in prices of gasoline, diesel and other fuels spurred investor enthusiasm Thursday for China's biggest oil companies, sending shares of fuel retailer China Petroleum & Chemical Corp., or Sinopec, up 9.4% in Hong Kong. The surprise announcement, made late Wednesday by China's top planning agency, was aimed at easing fuel shortages that have resulted from government price caps that kept fuel prices artificially low in the face of a global run-up in crude oil prices.
China's government had said as recently as last week that it wouldn't raise fuel prices because it was worried about fueling inflation. Underlining official caution about inflation, the government issued an estimate shortly after revealing the fuel-price increase saying it will have a limited impact on inflation, likely adding only 0.05 percentage point to the consumer-price index.
Still, analysts said that higher fuel prices could have a domino effect on other fuels, which could mean China's poorer citizens could be pinched tighter by bigger fuel and electricity bills. The National Development and Reform Commission, China's economic planning agency, also said Wednesday that it plans to adjust natural-gas prices soon so they are more in line with international markets.
Analysts said prices for coal, which can be used instead of natural gas to generate electricity or as a feedstock for chemicals, could also rise. If power plants see their coal prices going up, they could start lobbying to pass on the burden to consumers through higher electricity prices.
The government bowed to pressure after shortages first started appearing in China's export-oriented south and quickly spread inland. China last adjusted domestic fuel prices 17 months ago, keeping them flat in the interim while international crude prices rose 30%. That meant that domestic refiners like Sinopec were forced to buy crude oil at ever higher prices, and sell it at the same low, government-set ceiling. As losses widened, some refiners stopped producing fuel, while others started hoarding in anticipation of a price increase because they weren't allowed to pass through the high costs to the consumers. Crude-oil prices were down midday Thursday in New York at $93.45 but threaten to pass $100 a barrel.
Shares of Chinese-government controlled Sinopec rose to HK$12.76 (US$1.65) Thursday, up HK$1.10. Shares in PetroChina Co. rose 2.6% to HK$19.90 in Hong Kong.
In the third quarter, Sinopec's refineries posted an operating loss of 5.28 billion yuan (US$706 million), wiping out nearly all of the 5.49 billion yuan refining profit from the first half, when international oil prices were lower.
Analysts said that Chinese refiners will still lose money, and that the government may need to increase fuel prices further. Graham Cunningham, an analyst at Citigroup Inc., said that under the current regime, Sinopec is still losing $4 to $5 for every barrel of oil it refines, based on pretax earnings and current oil prices.
"This hike alone is not sufficient to restore profitability for the sector," Goldman Sachs Group Inc. said in a research report Thursday. "Unless crude oil prices fall to the low $80s, [China] would likely need to increase prices by another 15% to 20% in early 2008 if it wants to bring margins to above break-even levels to induce a supply response," the report said.
---
Shai Oster in Beijing and Aries Poon in Hong Kong contributed to this article.

Storm pushes up prices for crude oil and natural gas COMMODITIES MARKETPLACE by Bloomberg: [5 Edition]

Author: Tuttle, Robert

Publication info: Tuttle, Robert. 

https://ucd.idm.oclc.org/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/storm-pushes-up-prices-crude-oil-natural-gas/docview/318852992/se-2?accountid=14507

Abstract: Tropical Storm Dean was midway between Africa and the lesser Antilles and heading in the direction of the Gulf of Mexico, the center said. Links: https://JQ6AM9XS3S.search.serialssolutions.com?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Aeuropeannews&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=International+Herald+Tribune&amp;rft.atitle=Storm+pushes+up+prices+for+crude+oil+and+natural+gas+COMMODITIES+MARKETPLACE+by+Bloomberg%3A+%5B5+Edition%5D&amp;rft.au=Tuttle%2C+Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Tuttle&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.date=2007-08-15&amp;rft.volume=&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=13&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=International+Herald+Tribune&amp;rft.issn=02948052&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Prices for crude oil and natural gas rose Tuesday after the U.S. National Hurricane Center upgraded an Atlantic depression heading toward the Caribbean to a tropical storm.
Tropical Storm Dean was midway between Africa and the lesser Antilles and heading in the direction of the Gulf of Mexico, the center said. If the storm reaches "close to the coastline in the Gulf, it has the potential to not only impact platforms but also the pipeline infrastructure," said Eric Wittenauer, an energy industry analyst at A.G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis, Missouri. Crude oil for September delivery rose 76 cents to $72.38 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Nymex natural gas futures for October delivery rose 1.22 cents to $7.130 per million British thermal units. "It's simply a weather market right now," said Michael Haigh, senior commodity strategist for Societe Generale in New York.
Fuel prices rose to a record in 2005 after Hurricane Katrina decimated platforms, pipelines and refineries on the Mississippi and Louisiana coasts.
Other factors are propelling the increase, Haigh said. "We've got excessive heat in the South, Houston's boiling, and natural gas is very influenced by Texas and the South's weather, so any blip or change is going to create this volatility," he added.
Gold was little changed, with some dealers seeing a fall ahead in response to a stronger dollar, and others seeing possible gains. "Dollar strength is bringing gold lower," said Marty McNeill, a trader at R.F. Lafferty in New York. "It's dollar-related. The injection of funds helps keep the dollar steady." Gold futures for December delivery dipped $1.20 to $679.70 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
"Gold is still skittish about the amount of liquidity coming into the market," said Frank McGhee, head metals trader at Integrated Brokerage Services in Chicago.
"If the Fed has to continue to add liquidity to the system, then you've got a de facto rate cut and gold rallies. The real guess is how long the infusion stays in the system."
Credit: Bloomberg News
